style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a> text.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:Multilingual_support_(East_Asian)" title="Help:Multilingual support (East Asian)">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">Hangul</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">Hanja</a>.</td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<p><b>Korea</b> is an historic country in <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>; since 1945 it has been <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Korea" title="Division of Korea">divided</a> into two distinct <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign states</a>: <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a> (officially the "Democratic People\'s Republic of Korea") and <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> (officially the "Republic of Korea"). Located on the <a href="/wiki/Korean_Peninsula" title="Korean Peninsula">Korean Peninsula</a>, Korea is bordered by <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> to the northwest and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> to the northeast. It is separated from <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> to the east by the <a href="/wiki/Korea_Strait" title="Korea Strait">Korea Strait</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Japan" title="Sea of Japan">Sea of Japan</a> (East Sea).</p>\n<p>Korea emerged as a singular political entity in 936CE after centuries of conflict among the <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea" title="Three Kingdoms of Korea">Three Kingdoms of Korea</a>, which were unified as <a href="/wiki/Later_Silla" title="Later Silla">Later Silla</a> to the south and <a href="/wiki/Balhae" title="Balhae">Balhae</a> to the north. Later Silla divided into three separate states during the <a href="/wiki/Later_Three_Kingdoms_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Later Three Kingdoms period">Later Three Kingdoms period</a>. <a href="/wiki/Goryeo" title="Goryeo">Goryeo</a>, which had succeeded <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo" title="Goguryeo">Goguryeo</a>, defeated the two other states and united the <a href="/wiki/Korean_Peninsula" title="Korean Peninsula">Korean Peninsula</a>. Around the same time, Balhae collapsed and its last crown prince fled south to Goryeo. Goryeo (also spelled as <i>Kory\xc5\x8f</i>), whose name developed into the modern <a href="/wiki/Exonym" class="mw-redirect" title="Exonym">exonym</a> "Korea", was a highly cultured state that created the world\'s first metal <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">movable type</a> in 1234.<sup id="cite_ref-print_a_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-print_a-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-print_b_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-print_b-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-print_c_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-print_c-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-print_d_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-print_d-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-print_e_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-print_e-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-print_f_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-print_f-9">[9]</a></sup> However, multiple invasions by the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol</a> <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Yuan Dynasty">Yuan Dynasty</a> during the 13th century greatly weakened the nation, which eventually agreed to become a <a href="/wiki/Korea_under_Yuan_rule" title="Korea under Yuan rule">vassal state</a> after decades of fighting. Following the Yuan Dynasty\'s collapse, severe political strife followed, and Goryeo eventually fell to a coup led by General <a href="/wiki/Yi_Seong-gye" class="mw-redirect" title="Yi Seong-gye">Yi Seong-gye</a>, who established <a href="/wiki/Joseon" title="Joseon">Joseon</a> in 1392.</p>\n<p>The first 200 years of Joseon were marked by relative peace. During this period, the <a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">Korean</a> <a href="/wiki/Featural_alphabet" class="mw-redirect" title="Featural alphabet">alphabet</a> was created by <a href="/wiki/Sejong_the_Great" title="Sejong the Great">Sejong the Great</a> in the 15th century and there was increasing influence of <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a>. During the later part of the dynasty, Korea\'s isolationist policy earned it the Western nickname of the "<a href="/wiki/Hermit_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Hermit Kingdom">Hermit Kingdom</a>". By the late 19th century, the country became the object of imperial design by the Empire of Japan. After the <a href="/wiki/First_Sino-Japanese_War" title="First Sino-Japanese War">First Sino-Japanese War</a>, despite the <a href="/wiki/Korean_Empire" title="Korean Empire">Korean Empire</a>\'s effort to modernize, it was <a href="/wiki/Korea_under_Japanese_rule" title="Korea under Japanese rule">annexed by Japan</a> in 1910 and ruled by Imperial Japan until the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> in August 1945.</p>\n<p>In 1945, the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> agreed on the <a href="/wiki/Surrender_of_Japan" title="Surrender of Japan">surrender of Japanese forces</a> in Korea in the aftermath of World War II, leaving Korea partitioned along the 38th parallel. The <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Civil_Administration" title="Soviet Civil Administration">North was under Soviet occupation</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Military_Government_in_Korea" title="United States Army Military Government in Korea">South under U.S. occupation</a>. These circumstances soon became the basis for the division of Korea by the two <a href="/wiki/Superpower" title="Superpower">superpowers</a>, exacerbated by their incapability to agree on the terms of Korean independence. The Communist-inspired government in the North received backing from the Soviet Union in opposition to the pro-Western government in the South, leading to Korea\'s division into two political entities: North Korea (formally the Democratic People\'s Republic of Korea), and South Korea (formally the Republic of Korea). Tensions between the two resulted in the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a> in 1950. With involvement by foreign troops, the war ended in a stalemate in 1953, but without a formalized peace treaty. This status contributes to the high tensions that continue to divide the peninsula. To date, each country contends that it is the sole legitimate government of all of Korea; they refuse to recognize the other as legitimate.</p>\n<p></p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc">\n<div class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Notable_public_holidays_in_South_Korea"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Notable public holidays in South Korea</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<p></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Names_of_Korea" title="Names of Korea">Names of Korea</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Korean_romanization" class="mw-redirect" title="Korean romanization">Korean romanization</a></div>\n<p>"Korea" is the modern spelling of "Corea" (\xea\xb3\xa0\xeb\xa0\xa4), a name attested in English as early as 1614.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Korea was transliterated as <i>Cauli</i> in <a href="/wiki/The_Travels_of_Marco_Polo" title="The Travels of Marco Polo">The Travels of Marco Polo</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> based on the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Goryeo" title="Goryeo">Goryeo</a> (Kory\xc5\x8f), which ruled most of the Korean peninsula during Marco Polo\'s time. Korea\'s introduction to the West resulted from trade and contact with merchants from Arabic lands,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> with some records dating back as far as the 9th century.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Goryeo\'s name was a continuation of <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo" title="Goguryeo">Goguryeo</a> (Kogury\xc5\x8f) the northernmost of the <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea" title="Three Kingdoms of Korea">Three Kingdoms of Korea</a>, which was officially known as Goryeo beginning in the 5th century.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The original name was a combination of the adjective <i>go</i> ("high, lofty") with the name of a local <a href="/wiki/Yemaek" title="Yemaek">Yemaek</a> tribe, whose original name is thought to have been either *<span class="nowrap"><i>Guru</i> (<span lang="ko" xml:lang="ko">\xe6\xba\x9d\xe6\xa8\x93</span>,</span> "walled city," inferred from some toponyms in Chinese historical documents) or *<span class="nowrap"><i>Gauri</i> (<span lang="ko" xml:lang="ko">\xea\xb0\x80\xec\x9a\xb0\xeb\xa6\xac</span>,</span> "center"). With expanding British and American trade following the <a href="/wiki/Opening_of_Korea" class="mw-redirect" title="Opening of Korea">opening of Korea</a> in the late 19th century, the spelling "Korea" appeared and gradually grew in popularity; its use in transcribing <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="East Asian culture">East Asian</a> languages avoids the issues caused by the separate <a href="/wiki/Hard_and_soft_C" title="Hard and soft C">hard and soft Cs</a> existing in English vocabulary derived from the <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a>. The name Korea is now commonly used in English contexts by both North and South Korea.</p>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>, Korea as a whole is referred to as <span class="nowrap">Hanguk (<span lang="ko" xml:lang="ko">\xed\x95\x9c\xea\xb5\xad</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Korean" title="Help:IPA/Korean">[ha\xcb\x90n\xc9\xa1uk]</a></span>,</span> <small>lit.</small>\xe2\x80\x89"country of the <i><a href="/wiki/Samhan" title="Samhan">Han</a></i>"). The name references the <i><a href="/wiki/Samhan" title="Samhan">Samhan</a></i>\xe2\x80\x94<a href="/wiki/Mahan_confederacy" title="Mahan confederacy">Ma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jinhan" class="mw-redirect" title="Jinhan">Jin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Byeonhan" class="mw-redirect" title="Byeonhan">Byeon</a>\xe2\x80\x94who preceded the Three Kingdoms in the southern and central end of the peninsula during the 1st centuries\xe2\x80\x89<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span> and <span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span>. Although written in <a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">Hanja</a> as <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">\xe9\x9f\x93</span>, <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">\xe5\xb9\xb9</span>, or <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">\xe5\x88\x8a</span>, this <i>Han</i> has no relation to the Chinese place names or peoples who used those characters but was a <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Transcription_into_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Transcription into Chinese">phonetic transcription</a> (<small><a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">OC</a></small>:\xe2\x80\x89<a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_of_Old_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Reconstruction of Old Chinese">*</a><i>Gar</i>, <small><a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">MC</a></small>\xe2\x80\x89<i>Han</i><sup id="cite_ref-baxsag_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baxsag-14">[14]</a></sup></span> or <i>Gan</i>) of a native Korean word that seems to have had the meaning "big" or "great", particularly in reference to leaders. It has been tentatively linked with the title <i><a href="/wiki/Khan_(title)" title="Khan (title)">khan</a></i> used by the nomads of <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>.</p>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, and Chinese and Vietnamese-speaking areas, Korea as a whole is referred to as <span class="nowrap">Chos\xc5\x8fn (<span lang="ko" xml:lang="ko">\xec\xa1\xb0\xec\x84\xa0</span>, <i>Joseon</i>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Korean" title="Help:IPA/Korean">[t\xc9\x95os\xca\xb0\xca\x8cn]</a></span>,</span>, (<span lang="zh-Hans-CN" xml:lang="zh-Hans-CN">\xe6\x9c\x9d\xe9\xb2\x9c</span>), <i>Ch\xc3\xa1oxi\xc7\x8en</i>, (<span lang="ja-Hani" xml:lang="ja-Hani">\xe6\x9c\x9d\xe9\xae\xae</span>), <i>Ch\xc5\x8dsen</i>, <i><span lang="vi" xml:lang="vi">Tri\xe1\xbb\x81u Ti\xc3\xaan</span></i> (<span class="Unicode">\xe6\x9c\x9d\xe9\xae\xae</span>) <small>lit.</small>\xe2\x80\x89"[land of the] Morning Calm"). "Great <a href="/wiki/Joseon" title="Joseon">Joseon</a>" was the name of the kingdom ruled by the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Yi" title="House of Yi">Joseon dynasty</a> from 1393 until their declaration of the short-lived <a href="/wiki/Great_Korean_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Korean Empire">Great Korean Empire</a> in 1897. <a href="/wiki/Taejo_of_Joseon" title="Taejo of Joseon">King Taejo</a> had named them for the earlier <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Gojoseon" title="Gojoseon">Kojoseon</a> (<span lang="ko" xml:lang="ko">\xea\xb3\xa0\xec\xa1\xb0\xec\x84\xa0</span>),</span> who ruled northern Korea from its legendary <a href="/wiki/Prehistory_of_Korea" class="mw-redirect" title="Prehistory of Korea">prehistory</a> until their conquest in 108\xe2\x80\x89<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span> by China\'s <a href="/wiki/Han_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Han Empire">Han Empire</a>. This <i>go</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">Hanja</a> <span lang="ko" xml:lang="ko">\xe5\x8f\xa4</span> and simply means "ancient" or "old"; it is a modern usage to distinguish the ancient Joseon from the later dynasty. <i>Joseon</i> itself is the modern Korean pronunciation of <span class="nowrap">the <a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">Hanja</a> <span lang="ko" xml:lang="ko">\xe6\x9c\x9d\xe9\xae\xae</span></span> but it is unclear whether this was a <a href="/wiki/Transcription_into_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Transcription into Chinese">transcription</a> of a native Korean name (<small><a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">OC</a></small>\xe2\x80\x89<a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_of_Old_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Reconstruction of Old Chinese">*</a><i>T[r]awser</i>, <small><a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">MC</a></small>\xe2\x80\x89<i>Trjewsjen</i><sup id="cite_ref-baxsag_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baxsag-14">[14]</a></sup>) or a partial translation into Chinese of the Korean capital <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Asadal" title="Asadal">Asadal</a> (<span lang="ko" xml:lang="ko">\xec\x95\x84\xec\x82\xac\xeb\x8b\xac</span>), <sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></span> whose meaning has been reconstructed as "Morning Land" or "Mountain".</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote Korea</a></div>\n<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks vcard plainlist" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid period">Mumun</a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th style="padding:0.1em;background:#eee">Ancient</th>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<table href="/wiki/Jin_(Korean_state)" title="Jin (Korean state)">Jin state</a></td>\n<td style="white-space:nowrap;text-align:right;"></td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th style="padding:0.1em;background:#eee"><a href="/wiki/Proto%E2%80%93Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea" title="Proto\xe2\x80\x93Three Kingdoms of Korea">Proto\xe2\x80\x93Three Kingdoms</a></th>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="hlist">\n<ul>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Buyeo" title="Buyeo">Buyeo</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Goguryeo" title="Goguryeo">Goguryeo</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Okjeo" title="Okjeo">Okjeo</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Dongye" title="Dongye">Dongye</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Samhan" title="Samhan">Samhan</a>\n<ul>\n<li><span style="font-size:90%;"><a href="/wiki/Mahan_confederacy" title="Mahan confederacy">Ma</a></span></li>\n<li><span style="font-size:90%;"><a href="/wiki/Byeonhan_confederacy" title="Byeonhan confederacy">Byeon</a></span></li>\n<li><span style="font-size:90%;"><a href="/wiki/Jinhan_confederacy" title="Jinhan confederacy">Jin</a></span></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<ul>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Four_Commanderies_of_Han" title="Four Commanderies of Han">Han Commanderies</a></li>\n</ul>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th style="padding:0.1em;background:#eee"><a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea" title="Three Kingdoms of Korea">Three Kingdoms</a></th>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<table href="/wiki/Baekje" title="Baekje">Baekje</a></td>\n<td style="white-space:nowrap;text-align:right;">18 BC \xe2\x80\x93 660 AD</td>\n</tr>\n<tr style="vertical-align:top">\n<td style="text-align:left;"><a href="/wiki/Silla" title="Silla">Silla</a></td>\n<td style="white-space:nowrap;text-align:right;">57 BC \xe2\x80\x93 935 AD</td>\n</tr>\n<tr style="white-space:nowrap;text-align:right;">1910\xe2\x80\x931945</td>\n</tr>\n<tr style="vertical-align:top">\n<td style="text-align:left;"><a style="white-space:nowrap;text-align:right;">1919\xe2\x80\x931948</td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th style="padding:0.1em;background:#eee"><a href="/wiki/Division_of_Korea" title="Division of Korea">Division of Korea</a></th>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<table style="width:100%;border-collapse:collapse;border-spacing:0px 0px;border:none">\n<tr style="text-align:left;"><a href="/wiki/History_of_South_Korea" title="History of South Korea">South Korea</a></td>\n<td style="white-space:nowrap;text-align:right;">1948\xe2\x80\x93<span style="font-size:90%;">present</span></td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th style="padding:0.1em;background:#eee">By topic</th>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="hlist">\n<ul>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Korean_art#History" title="Korean art">Art</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Korean_language" class="mw-redirect" title="History of the Korean language">Language</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_Korea" title="Military history of Korea">Military</a> (<a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_Goguryeo" title="Military history of Korea">Naval</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_science_and_technology_in_Korea" title="History of science and technology in Korea">Science and technology</a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em;padding:0.2em; border-top:#aaa 1px solid;"><b><a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Korean_history" title="Timeline of Korean history">Timeline</a></b></td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top:#aaa 1px solid; border-bottom:#aaa 1px solid;"><a href="/wiki/File:Korea_Map.svg" class="image"><img alt="Korea Map.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Korea_Map.svg/16px-Korea_Map.svg.png" width="16" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Korea_Map.svg/24px-Korea_Map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Korea_Map.svg/32px-Korea_Map.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="64" data-file-height="64" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Korea" title="Portal:Korea">Korea portal</a></td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%">\n<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">\n<ul>\n<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_Korea" title="Template:History of Korea"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>\n<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_Korea" title="Template talk:History of Korea"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>\n<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_Korea&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory_and_Gojoseon">Prehistory and Gojoseon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Prehistory and Gojoseon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Korea" title="Prehistoric Korea">Prehistoric Korea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gojoseon" title="Gojoseon">Gojoseon</a></div>\n<p>The Korean Academy claimed ancient hominid fossils originating from about 100,000\xe2\x80\x89<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span> in the lava at a stone city site in Korea. Fluorescent and high-magnetic analyses indicate the volcanic fossils may be from as early as 300,000\xe2\x80\x89<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The best preserved Korean pottery goes back to the <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">paleolithic</a> times around 10,000\xe2\x80\x89<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span> and the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> period begins around 6000\xe2\x80\x89<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span>.</p>\n<p>According to legend, <a href="/wiki/Dangun" title="Dangun">Dangun</a>, a descendant of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Cheon&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Cheon (page does not exist)">Heaven</a>, established <a href="/wiki/Gojoseon" title="Gojoseon">Gojoseon</a> in 2333\xe2\x80\x89<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span>. In 108\xe2\x80\x89<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span>, the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> defeated Gojoseon and installed <a href="/wiki/Four_Commanderies_of_Han" title="Four Commanderies of Han">four commanderies</a> in the northern Korean peninsula. Three of the commanderies fell or retreated westward within a few decades, but the <a href="/wiki/Lelang_commandery" class="mw-redirect" title="Lelang commandery">Lelang commandery</a> remained as a center of cultural and economic exchange with successive Chinese dynasties for four centuries. By 313, <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo" title="Goguryeo">Goguryeo</a> annexed all of the Chinese commanderies.</p>\n<h3><span id="Proto\xe2\x80\x93Three_Kingdoms"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Proto.E2.80.93Three_Kingdoms">Proto\xe2\x80\x93Three Kingdoms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Proto\xe2\x80\x93Three Kingdoms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Proto%E2%80%93Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea" title="Proto\xe2\x80\x93Three Kingdoms of Korea">Proto\xe2\x80\x93Three Kingdoms of Korea</a></div>\n<p>The Proto\xe2\x80\x93Three Kingdoms period, sometimes called the Multiple States Period, is the earlier part of what is commonly called the <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea" title="Three Kingdoms of Korea">Three Kingdoms Period</a>, following the fall of <a href="/wiki/Gojoseon" title="Gojoseon">Gojoseon</a> but before <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo" title="Goguryeo">Goguryeo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baekje" title="Baekje">Baekje</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Silla" title="Silla">Silla</a> fully developed into kingdoms.</p>\n<p>This time period saw numerous states spring up from the former territories of Gojoseon, which encompassed northern Korea and southern <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a>. With the fall of Gojoseon, southern Korea entered the <a href="/wiki/Samhan" title="Samhan">Samhan</a> period.</p>\n<p>Located in the southern part of the <a href="/wiki/Korean_Peninsula" title="Korean Peninsula">Korean Peninsula</a>, Samhan refers to the three confederacies of <a href="/wiki/Mahan_confederacy" title="Mahan confederacy">Mahan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jinhan_confederacy" title="Jinhan confederacy">Jinhan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Byeonhan_confederacy" title="Byeonhan confederacy">Byeonhan</a>. Mahan was the largest and consisted of 54 states. <a href="/wiki/Byeonhan" class="mw-redirect" title="Byeonhan">Byeonhan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jinhan" class="mw-redirect" title="Jinhan">Jinhan</a> both consisted of twelve states, bringing a total of 78 states within the <a href="/wiki/Samhan" title="Samhan">Samhan</a>. These three confederacies eventually developed into <a href="/wiki/Baekje" title="Baekje">Baekje</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silla" title="Silla">Silla</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gaya_confederacy" title="Gaya confederacy">Gaya</a>.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Three_Kingdoms">Three Kingdoms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Three Kingdoms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea" title="Three Kingdoms of Korea">Three Kingdoms of Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baekje" title="Baekje">Baekje</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo" title="Goguryeo">Goguryeo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Silla" title="Silla">Silla</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:7th_century_painting_of_Koreans.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/ec/7th_century_painting_of_Koreans.png/220px-7th_century_painting_of_Koreans.png" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/ec/7th_century_painting_of_Koreans.png/330px-7th_century_painting_of_Koreans.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/ec/7th_century_painting_of_Koreans.png/440px-7th_century_painting_of_Koreans.png 2x" data-file-width="4200" data-file-height="2784" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:7th_century_painting_of_Koreans.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n7th century Tang dynasty painting of envoys from the Three Kingdoms of Korea: Baekje, Goguryeo, and Silla</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea" title="Three Kingdoms of Korea">Three Kingdoms of Korea</a> consisted of <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo" title="Goguryeo">Goguryeo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silla" title="Silla">Silla</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Baekje" title="Baekje">Baekje</a>. Silla and Baekje controlled the southern half of the <a href="/wiki/Korean_Peninsula" title="Korean Peninsula">Korean Peninsula</a>, maintaining the former <a href="/wiki/Samhan" title="Samhan">Samhan</a> territories, while Goguryeo controlled the northern half of the Korean Peninsula, Manchuria and the <a href="/wiki/Liaodong_Peninsula" title="Liaodong Peninsula">Liaodong Peninsula</a>, uniting <a href="/wiki/Buyeo_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Buyeo Kingdom">Buyeo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Okjeo" title="Okjeo">Okjeo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dongye" title="Dongye">Dongye</a>, and other states in the former <a href="/wiki/Gojoseon" title="Gojoseon">Gojoseon</a> territories.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Goguryeo" title="Goguryeo">Goguryeo</a> was a highly militaristic state;<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> it was a powerful empire and one of the great powers in <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Walker_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walker-23">[23]</a></sup> reaching its zenith in the 5th century when its territories expanded to encompass most of Manchuria to the north, parts of <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a> to the west,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> parts of Russia to the east,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and the Seoul region to the south.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Goguryeo experienced a golden age under <a href="/wiki/Gwanggaeto_the_Great" title="Gwanggaeto the Great">Gwanggaeto the Great</a> and his son <a href="/wiki/Jangsu_of_Goguryeo" title="Jangsu of Goguryeo">Jangsu</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> who both subdued Baekje and Silla during their times, achieving a brief unification of the <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea" title="Three Kingdoms of Korea">Three Kingdoms of Korea</a> and becoming the most dominant power on the Korean Peninsula.<sup id="cite_ref-Jinwung_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jinwung-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> In addition to contesting for control of the Korean Peninsula, Goguryeo had many <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_Goguryeo" title="Military history of Goguryeo">military conflicts</a> with various Chinese dynasties,<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> most notably the <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo-Sui_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Goguryeo-Sui War">Goguryeo-Sui War</a>, in which Goguryeo defeated a huge force said to number over a million men.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nahm_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nahm-38">[38]</a></sup> In 642, the powerful general <a href="/wiki/Yeon_Gaesomun" title="Yeon Gaesomun">Yeon Gaesomun</a> led a coup and gained complete control over Goguryeo. In response, Emperor <a href="/wiki/Tang_Taizong" class="mw-redirect" title="Tang Taizong">Tang Taizong</a> of China led a <a href="/wiki/First_campaign_in_the_Goguryeo%E2%80%93Tang_War" title="First campaign in the Goguryeo\xe2\x80\x93Tang War">campaign against Goguryeo</a>, but was defeated and retreated.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> After the death of Tang Taizong, his son Emperor <a href="/wiki/Tang_Gaozong" class="mw-redirect" title="Tang Gaozong">Tang Gaozong</a> allied with the Korean kingdom of Silla and invaded Goguryeo again, but was unable to overcome Goguryeo\'s stalwart defenses and was defeated in 662.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Maps_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maps-42">[42]</a></sup> However, Yeon Gaesomun died of a natural cause in 666 and Goguryeo was thrown into chaos and weakened by a succession struggle among his sons and younger brother, with his eldest son defecting to <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang</a> and his younger brother defecting to Silla.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The Tang-Silla alliance finally conquered Goguryeo in 668. After the collapse of Goguryeo, Tang and Silla ended their alliance and fought over control of the Korean Peninsula. Silla succeeded in gaining control over most of the Korean Peninsula, while Tang gained control over Goguryeo\'s northern territories. However, 30 years after the fall of Goguryeo, a Goguryeo general by the name of <a href="/wiki/Dae_Joyeong" class="mw-redirect" title="Dae Joyeong">Dae Joyeong</a> founded the Korean-Mohe state of <a href="/wiki/Balhae" title="Balhae">Balhae</a> and successfully expelled the Tang presence from much of the former Goguryeo territories.</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Seokguram_Buddha.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Seokguram_Buddha.JPG/170px-Seokguram_Buddha.JPG" width="170" height="232" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Seokguram_Buddha.JPG/255px-Seokguram_Buddha.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Seokguram_Buddha.JPG/340px-Seokguram_Buddha.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1091" data-file-height="1488" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Seokguram_Buddha.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/Seokguram_Grotto" class="mw-redirect" title="Seokguram Grotto">Seokguram Grotto</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Silla" title="Silla">Silla</a> era, a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_Site" class="mw-redirect" title="UNESCO World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The southwestern Korean kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Baekje" title="Baekje">Baekje</a> was founded around modern-day <a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">Seoul</a> by a <a href="/wiki/Onjo_of_Baekje" title="Onjo of Baekje">Goguryeo prince</a>, a son of the <a href="/wiki/Dongmyeong_of_Goguryeo" title="Dongmyeong of Goguryeo">founder of Goguryeo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Baekje absorbed all of the <a href="/wiki/Mahan_confederacy" title="Mahan confederacy">Mahan</a> states and subjugated most of the western Korean peninsula (including the modern provinces of <a href="/wiki/Gyeonggi" class="mw-redirect" title="Gyeonggi">Gyeonggi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chungcheong-do" class="mw-redirect" title="Chungcheong-do">Chungcheong</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jeolla" class="mw-redirect" title="Jeolla">Jeolla</a>, as well as parts of <a href="/wiki/Hwanghae" class="mw-redirect" title="Hwanghae">Hwanghae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gangwon-do_(South_Korea)" class="mw-redirect" title="Gangwon-do (South Korea)">Gangwon</a>) to a centralised government; during the expansion of its territory, Baekje acquired Chinese culture and technology through maritime contacts with the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Dynasties" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern Dynasties">Southern Dynasties</a>. Baekje was a great maritime power;<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> its nautical skill, which made it the <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a> of East Asia, was instrumental in the dissemination of Buddhism throughout East Asia and continental culture to Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Historic evidence suggests that Japanese culture, art, and language were influenced by the kingdom of Baekje and Korea itself;<sup id="cite_ref-Walker_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walker-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-www2.kenyon.edu_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-www2.kenyon.edu-60">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Baekje also played an important role in transmitting advanced Chinese culture to the Japanese archipelago. Baekje was once a great military power on the Korean Peninsula, most notably in the 4th century during the rule of <a href="/wiki/Geunchogo_of_Baekje" title="Geunchogo of Baekje">Geunchogo</a> when its influence extended across the sea to <a href="/wiki/Liaoxi" class="mw-redirect" title="Liaoxi">Liaoxi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shandong" title="Shandong">Shandong</a> in China, taking advantage of the weakened state of <a href="/wiki/Former_Qin" title="Former Qin">Former Qin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kyushu" title="Kyushu">Kyushu</a> in the Japanese archipelago;<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> however, Baekje was critically defeated by Gwanggaeto the Great and declined.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Although later records claim that <a href="/wiki/Silla" title="Silla">Silla</a> was the oldest of the <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms_of_Korea" title="Three Kingdoms of Korea">Three Kingdoms of Korea</a>, it is now believed to have been the last kingdom to develop. By the 2nd century, Silla existed as a large state in the southeast, occupying and influencing its neighboring city-states. In 562, Silla annexed the <a href="/wiki/Gaya_confederacy" title="Gaya confederacy">Gaya confederacy</a> which was located between Baekje and Silla. The Three Kingdoms of Korea often warred with each other and Silla was often dominated by Baekje and Goguryeo. Silla was the smallest and weakest of the three, but it used cunning diplomatic means to make opportunistic pacts and alliances with the more powerful Korean kingdoms, and eventually Tang China, to its great advantage.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> In 660, King <a href="/wiki/Muyeol_of_Silla" title="Muyeol of Silla">Muyeol</a> ordered his armies to attack <a href="/wiki/Baekje" title="Baekje">Baekje</a>. General <a href="/wiki/Kim_Yu-shin" class="mw-redirect" title="Kim Yu-shin">Kim Yu-shin</a>, aided by <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang</a> forces, conquered Baekje after defeating General <a href="/wiki/Gyebaek" title="Gyebaek">Gyebaek</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hwangsanbeol" title="Battle of Hwangsanbeol">Battle of Hwangsanbeol</a>. In 661, Silla and Tang attacked Goguryeo but were repelled. King <a href="/wiki/Munmu_of_Silla" title="Munmu of Silla">Munmu</a>, son of Muyeol and nephew of General Kim Yu-shin, launched another campaign in 667 and Goguryeo fell in the following year.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North-South_States_Period">North-South States Period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: North-South States Period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/North_South_States_Period" class="mw-redirect" title="North South States Period">North South States Period</a>, <a href="/wiki/Later_Silla" title="Later Silla">Later Silla</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Balhae" title="Balhae">Balhae</a></div>\n<p>Beginning in the 6th century, <a href="/wiki/Silla" title="Silla">Silla</a>\'s power gradually extended across the Korean Peninsula. Silla first annexed the adjacent <a href="/wiki/Gaya_confederacy" title="Gaya confederacy">Gaya confederacy</a> in 562. By the 640s, Silla formed an alliance with the <a href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Tang Dynasty">Tang Dynasty</a> of China to conquer <a href="/wiki/Baekje" title="Baekje">Baekje</a> and later <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo" title="Goguryeo">Goguryeo</a>. After conquering Baekje and Goguryeo, Silla repulsed Tang China from the Korean peninsula in 676. Even though Silla unified most of the Korean Peninsula, most of the Goguryeo territories to the north of the Korean Peninsula were ruled by <a href="/wiki/Balhae" title="Balhae">Balhae</a>. Former Goguryeo general <a href="/wiki/Dae_Jo-yeong" class="mw-redirect" title="Dae Jo-yeong">Dae Jo-yeong</a> led a group of Goguryeo and <a href="/wiki/Mohe_people" title="Mohe people">Mohe</a> refugees to the <a href="/wiki/Jilin" title="Jilin">Jilin</a> and founded the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Balhae" title="Balhae">Balhae</a>, 30 years after the collapse of Goguryeo, as the successor to Goguryeo. At its height, Balhae\'s territories extended from southern <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a> down to the northern Korean peninsula. Balhae was called the "Prosperous Country in the East".<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Later_Silla" title="Later Silla">Later Silla</a> carried on the maritime prowess of <a href="/wiki/Baekje" title="Baekje">Baekje</a>, which acted like the <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a> of medieval <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> and during the 8th and 9th centuries dominated the seas of East Asia and the trade between China, Korea and Japan, most notably during the time of <a href="/wiki/Jang_Bogo" title="Jang Bogo">Jang Bogo</a>; in addition, Silla people made overseas communities in China on the <a href="/wiki/Shandong_Peninsula" title="Shandong Peninsula">Shandong Peninsula</a> and the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Yangtze_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Yangtze River">Yangtze River</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Later Silla was a prosperous and wealthy country,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> and its metropolitan capital of <a href="/wiki/Gyeongju" title="Gyeongju">Gyeongju</a><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> was the fourth largest city in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Later Silla was a golden age of art and culture,<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> as evidenced by the <a href="/wiki/Hwangnyongsa" title="Hwangnyongsa">Hwangnyongsa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seokguram" title="Seokguram">Seokguram</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Emille_Bell" class="mw-redirect" title="Emille Bell">Emille Bell</a>. Buddhism flourished during this time, and many Korean Buddhists gained great fame among Chinese Buddhists<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> and contributed to Chinese Buddhism,<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> including: <a href="/wiki/Woncheuk" title="Woncheuk">Woncheuk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wonhyo" title="Wonhyo">Wonhyo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uisang" title="Uisang">Uisang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kim_Hwasang" title="Kim Hwasang">Musang</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Kim_Gyo-gak" title="Kim Gyo-gak">Kim Gyo-gak</a>, a Silla prince whose influence made <a href="/wiki/Mount_Jiuhua" title="Mount Jiuhua">Mount Jiuhua</a> one of the Four <a href="/wiki/Sacred_Mountains_of_China" title="Sacred Mountains of China">Sacred Mountains</a> of Chinese Buddhism.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Later Silla fell apart in the late 9th century, giving way to the tumultuous <a href="/wiki/Later_Three_Kingdoms_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Later Three Kingdoms period">Later Three Kingdoms period</a> (892\xe2\x80\x93935), and Balhae was destroyed by the <a href="/wiki/Khitan_people" title="Khitan people">Khitans</a> in 926. <a href="/wiki/Goryeo" title="Goryeo">Goryeo</a> unified the Later Three Kingdoms and received the <a href="/wiki/Dae_Gwang-hyeon" title="Dae Gwang-hyeon">last crown prince</a> and much of the ruling class of Balhae, thus bringing about a unification of the two successor nations of <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo" title="Goguryeo">Goguryeo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Balhae_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balhae-93">[93]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Goryeo_dynasty">Goryeo dynasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Goryeo dynasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Goryeo" title="Goryeo">Goryeo</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Goryeo" title="Goryeo">Goryeo</a> was founded in 918 and replaced Silla as the ruling dynasty of Korea. Goryeo\'s land was at first what is now South Korea and about 1/3 of North Korea, but later on managed to recover most of the Korean peninsula. Momentarily, Goryeo advanced to parts of <a href="/wiki/Jiandao" title="Jiandao">Jiandao</a> while conquering the <a href="/wiki/Jurchen_people" title="Jurchen people">Jurchens</a>, but returned the territories due to the harsh climate and difficulties in defending them. The name "Goryeo" (\xe9\xab\x98\xe9\xba\x97) is a short form of "<a href="/wiki/Goguryeo" title="Goguryeo">Goguryeo</a>" (\xe9\xab\x98\xe5\x8f\xa5\xe9\xba\x97) and was first used during the time of King <a href="/wiki/Jangsu_of_Goguryeo" title="Jangsu of Goguryeo">Jangsu</a>. Goryeo regarded itself as the successor of Goguryeo, hence its name and efforts to recover the former territories of Goguryeo.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wang_Geon" class="mw-redirect" title="Wang Geon">Wang Geon</a>, the founder of Goryeo, was of Goguryeo descent,<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> and traced his ancestry to a noble Goguryeo clan.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> He made <a href="/wiki/Kaesong" title="Kaesong">Kaesong</a>, his hometown, the capital.</p>\n<p>During this period, laws were codified and a civil service system was introduced. <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> flourished and spread throughout the peninsula. The development of <a href="/wiki/Goryeo_ware" title="Goryeo ware">celadon</a> industries flourished in the 12th and 13th centuries. The publication of the <a href="/wiki/Tripitaka_Koreana" title="Tripitaka Koreana">Tripitaka Koreana</a> onto more than 80,000 wooden blocks and the invention of the world\'s first metal <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">movable type</a> in the 13th century attest to Goryeo\'s cultural achievements.<sup id="cite_ref-print_a_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-print_a-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-print_b_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-print_b-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-print_c_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-print_c-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-print_d_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-print_d-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-print_e_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-print_e-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-print_f_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-print_f-9">[9]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Goryeo had to defend frequently against attacks by nomadic empires, especially the <a href="/wiki/Khitan_people" title="Khitan people">Khitans</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a>. Goryeo had a hostile relationship with the Khitans, because the <a href="/wiki/Khitan_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Khitan Empire">Khitan Empire</a> had destroyed <a href="/wiki/Balhae" title="Balhae">Balhae</a>, also a successor state of Goguryeo. In 993, the Khitans, who had established the <a href="/wiki/Liao_dynasty" title="Liao dynasty">Liao dynasty</a> in 907, <a href="/wiki/First_conflict_in_the_Goryeo%E2%80%93Khitan_War" title="First conflict in the Goryeo\xe2\x80\x93Khitan War">invaded Goryeo</a>, demanding that it make amity with them. Goryeo sent the diplomat <a href="/wiki/Seo_Hui" title="Seo Hui">Seo Hui</a> to negotiate, who successfully persuaded the Khitans to let Goryeo expand to the banks of the <a href="/wiki/Yalu_River" title="Yalu River">Amnok River</a>, citing that in the past the land belonged to Goguryeo, the predecessor of Goryeo.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Goryeo%E2%80%93Khitan_War" title="Goryeo\xe2\x80\x93Khitan War">Goryeo\xe2\x80\x93Khitan War</a>, the Khitan Empire invaded Korea twice more in <a href="/wiki/Second_conflict_in_the_Goryeo%E2%80%93Khitan_War" title="Second conflict in the Goryeo\xe2\x80\x93Khitan War">1009</a> and <a href="/wiki/Third_conflict_in_the_Goryeo%E2%80%93Khitan_War" title="Third conflict in the Goryeo\xe2\x80\x93Khitan War">1018</a>, but was defeated.</p>\n<p>After defeating the Khitan Empire, which was the most powerful empire of its time,<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Goryeo experienced a golden age that lasted a century, during which the <a href="/wiki/Tripitaka_Koreana" title="Tripitaka Koreana">Tripitaka Koreana</a> was completed, and there were great developments in printing and publishing, promoting learning and dispersing knowledge on philosophy, literature, religion, and science; by 1100, there were 12 universities that produced famous scholars and scientists.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Goryeo was <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_of_Korea" title="Mongol invasions of Korea">invaded by the Mongols</a> in seven major campaigns from the 1230s until the 1270s, but was never conquered.<sup id="cite_ref-Mongols_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mongols-105">[105]</a></sup> Exhausted after decades of fighting, Goryeo sent its <a href="/wiki/Wonjong_of_Goryeo" title="Wonjong of Goryeo">crown prince</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan</a> capital to swear allegiance to the Mongols; <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a> accepted, and married one of his daughters to the Korean crown prince,<sup id="cite_ref-Mongols_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mongols-105">[105]</a></sup> and the dynastic line of Goryeo continued to survive <a href="/wiki/Korea_under_Yuan_rule" title="Korea under Yuan rule">under the overlordship</a> of the Mongol Yuan dynasty as a semi-autonomous vassal state and compulsory ally. The two nations became intertwined for 80 years as all subsequent Korean kings married Mongol princesses,<sup id="cite_ref-Mongols_105-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mongols-105">[105]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Empress_Gi" title="Empress Gi">last empress</a> of the Yuan dynasty was a Korean princess.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In the 1350s, <a href="/wiki/Gongmin_of_Goryeo" title="Gongmin of Goryeo">King Gongmin</a> was free at last to reform the Goryeo government when the Yuan dynasty began to crumble. Gongmin had various problems that needed to be dealt with, which included the removal of pro-Mongol aristocrats and military officials, the question of land holding, and quelling the growing animosity between the Buddhists and <a href="/wiki/Confucian" class="mw-redirect" title="Confucian">Confucian</a> scholars. During this tumultuous period, Goryeo momentarily conquered <a href="/wiki/Liaoyang" title="Liaoyang">Liaoyang</a> in 1356, repulsed two large <a href="/wiki/Red_Turban_invasions_of_Goryeo" title="Red Turban invasions of Goryeo">invasions by the Red Turbans</a> in 1359 and 1360, and defeated the final attempt by the Yuan to dominate Goryeo when General <a href="/wiki/Choe_Yeong" title="Choe Yeong">Choe Yeong</a> defeated a Mongol <a href="/wiki/Tumen_(unit)" title="Tumen (unit)">tumen</a> in 1364. During the 1380s, Goryeo turned its attention to the <a href="/wiki/Wokou" title="Wokou">Wokou</a> threat and used <a href="/wiki/Naval_history_of_Korea" title="Naval history of Korea">naval artillery</a> created by <a href="/wiki/Choe_Museon" title="Choe Museon">Choe Museon</a> to annihilate hundreds of pirate ships.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Joseon_dynasty">Joseon dynasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Joseon dynasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Joseon" title="Joseon">Joseon</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gyeongbok-gung_palace-05_(xndr).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Gyeongbok-gung_palace-05_%28xndr%29.jpg/220px-Gyeongbok-gung_palace-05_%28xndr%29.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Gyeongbok-gung_palace-05_%28xndr%29.jpg/330px-Gyeongbok-gung_palace-05_%28xndr%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Gyeongbok-gung_palace-05_%28xndr%29.jpg/440px-Gyeongbok-gung_palace-05_%28xndr%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gyeongbok-gung_palace-05_(xndr).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe <a href="/wiki/Gyeongbokgung" title="Gyeongbokgung">Gyeongbokgung</a> Palace</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Joseon_plate_mail_in_Gyeongbokgung_Palace.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Joseon_plate_mail_in_Gyeongbokgung_Palace.jpg/220px-Joseon_plate_mail_in_Gyeongbokgung_Palace.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Joseon_plate_mail_in_Gyeongbokgung_Palace.jpg/330px-Joseon_plate_mail_in_Gyeongbokgung_Palace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Joseon_plate_mail_in_Gyeongbokgung_Palace.jpg/440px-Joseon_plate_mail_in_Gyeongbokgung_Palace.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="1280" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Joseon_plate_mail_in_Gyeongbokgung_Palace.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nKorean <a href="/wiki/Plated_mail" class="mw-redirect" title="Plated mail">plated mail</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>In 1392, the general <a href="/wiki/Taejo_of_Joseon" title="Taejo of Joseon">Yi Seong-gye</a> overthrew the <a href="/wiki/Goryeo" title="Goryeo">Goryeo</a> dynasty after he staged a coup and defeated General <a href="/wiki/Choe_Yeong" title="Choe Yeong">Choe Yeong</a>. Yi Seong-gye named his new dynasty <a href="/wiki/Joseon" title="Joseon">Joseon</a> and moved the capital from <a href="/wiki/Kaesong" title="Kaesong">Kaesong</a> to <a href="/wiki/Hanseong" class="mw-redirect" title="Hanseong">Hanseong</a> (formerly Hanyang; modern-day <a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">Seoul</a>) and built the <a href="/wiki/Gyeongbokgung" title="Gyeongbokgung">Gyeongbokgung</a> palace.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> In 1394, he adopted <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a> as the country\'s official ideology, resulting in much loss of power and wealth by the <a href="/wiki/Korean_Buddhism" title="Korean Buddhism">Buddhists</a>. The prevailing philosophy of the Joseon dynasty was <a href="/wiki/Korean_Confucianism" title="Korean Confucianism">Neo-Confucianism</a>, which was epitomized by the <a href="/wiki/Seonbi" title="Seonbi">seonbi</a> class, scholars who passed up positions of wealth and power to lead lives of study and integrity.</p>\n<p>Joseon was a nominal tributary state of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> but exercised full sovereignty,<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> and maintained the highest position among China\'s tributary states,<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> which also included countries such as the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Kingdom" title="Ryukyu Kingdom">Ryukyu Kingdom</a>, Vietnam, Thailand,<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> the Philippines, Burma, Laos, among others.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> In addition, Joseon received tribute from Jurchens and Japanese until the 17th century,<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> and had a small enclave in the Ryukyu Kingdom that engaged in trade with Siam and Java.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>\n<p>During the 15th and 16th centuries, Joseon enjoyed many benevolent rulers who promoted education and science.<sup id="cite_ref-JoseonGold_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JoseonGold-121">[121]</a></sup> Most notable among them was <a href="/wiki/Sejong_the_Great" title="Sejong the Great">Sejong the Great</a> (r. 1418\xe2\x80\x9350), who promulgated <a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">Hangul</a>, the Korean alphabet. This golden age<sup id="cite_ref-JoseonGold_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JoseonGold-121">[121]</a></sup> saw great cultural and scientific advancements,<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> including in printing, <a href="/wiki/Rain_gauge" title="Rain gauge">meteorological observation</a>, astronomy, calendar science, <a href="/wiki/Korean_pottery_and_porcelain" title="Korean pottery and porcelain">ceramics</a>, military technology, geography, cartography, medicine, and agricultural technology, some of which were unrivaled elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Joseon implemented a class system that consisted of <i><a href="/wiki/Yangban" title="Yangban">yangban</a></i> the noble class, <i>jungin</i> the middle class, <i>yangin</i> the common class, and <i>cheonin</i> the lowest class, which included occupations such as butchers, tanners, shamans, entertainers, and <i><a href="/wiki/Nobi" title="Nobi">nobi</a></i>, the equivalent of slaves, bondservants, or <a href="/wiki/Serfs" class="mw-redirect" title="Serfs">serfs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In 1592 and again in 1597, the <a href="/wiki/Imjin_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Imjin War">Japanese invaded Korea</a>; the Korean military at the time was unprepared and untrained, due to two centuries of peace on the <a href="/wiki/Korean_Peninsula" title="Korean Peninsula">Korean Peninsula</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Toyotomi_Hideyoshi" title="Toyotomi Hideyoshi">Toyotomi Hideyoshi</a> intended to conquer <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> through the Korean Peninsula, but was defeated by strong resistance from the <a href="/wiki/Righteous_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="Righteous Army">Righteous Army</a>, the naval superiority of Admiral <a href="/wiki/Yi_Sun-sin" title="Yi Sun-sin">Yi Sun-sin</a> and his <a href="/wiki/Turtle_ship" title="Turtle ship">turtle ships</a>, and assistance from <a href="/wiki/Wanli_Emperor" title="Wanli Emperor">Wanli Emperor</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming China</a>. However, Joseon experienced great destruction, including a tremendous loss of cultural sites such as temples and palaces to Japanese pillaging, and the Japanese brought back to Japan an estimated 100,000\xe2\x80\x93200,000 <a href="/wiki/Nose_tomb" title="Nose tomb">noses</a> cut from Korean victims.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Less than 30 years after the Japanese invasions, the <a href="/wiki/Manchus" class="mw-redirect" title="Manchus">Manchus</a> took advantage of Joseon\'s war-weakened state and <a href="/wiki/Manchu_invasion_of_Korea" title="Manchu invasion of Korea">invaded</a> in 1627 and 1637, and then went on to <a href="/wiki/Qing_conquest_of_the_Ming" title="Qing conquest of the Ming">conquer</a> the destabilized Ming dynasty.</p>\n<p>After normalizing relations with the new <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a>, Joseon experienced a nearly 200-year period of peace. Kings <a href="/wiki/Yeongjo_of_Joseon" title="Yeongjo of Joseon">Yeongjo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jeongjo_of_Joseon" title="Jeongjo of Joseon">Jeongjo</a> led a new renaissance of the Joseon dynasty during the 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In the 19th century, the royal in-law families gained control of the government, leading to mass corruption and weakening of the state, and severe poverty and peasant rebellions throughout the country. Furthermore, the Joseon government adopted a strict isolationist policy, earning the nickname "the <a href="/wiki/Hermit_kingdom" title="Hermit kingdom">hermit kingdom</a>", but ultimately failed to protect itself against <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialism</a> and was forced to open its borders, beginning an era leading into <a href="/wiki/Korea_under_Japanese_rule" title="Korea under Japanese rule">Japanese imperial rule</a>.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Korean_Empire">Korean Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Korean Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Korean_Empire" title="Korean Empire">Korean Empire</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Taegukgi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Taegukgi.jpg/220px-Taegukgi.jpg" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/97/Taegukgi.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="216" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Taegukgi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe earliest surviving depiction of the Korean flag was printed in a US Navy book <i>Flags of Maritime Nations</i> in July 1889.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Beginning in 1871, <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Japan</a> began to exert more influence in Korea, forcing it out of China\'s traditional sphere of influence. As a result of the Sino-Japanese War (1894\xe2\x80\x9395), the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a> had to give up such a position according to Article 1 of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Shimonoseki" title="Treaty of Shimonoseki">Treaty of Shimonoseki</a>, which was concluded between China and Japan in 1895. That same year, <a href="/wiki/Empress_Myeongseong" title="Empress Myeongseong">Empress Myeongseong</a> of Korea was assassinated by Japanese agents.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-131">[131]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In 1897, the <a href="/wiki/Joseon_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseon dynasty">Joseon dynasty</a> proclaimed the <a href="/wiki/Korean_Empire" title="Korean Empire">Korean Empire</a> (1897\xe2\x80\x931910). <a href="/wiki/Gojong_of_the_Korean_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Gojong of the Korean Empire">King Gojong</a> became an <a href="/wiki/Gojong_of_the_Korean_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Gojong of the Korean Empire">emperor</a>. During this brief period, Korea had some success in modernizing the military, economy, real property laws, education system, and various industries. <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russia</a>, Japan, <a href="/wiki/French_Third_Republic" title="French Third Republic">France</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> all invested in the country and sought to influence it politically.</p>\n<p>In 1904, the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Japanese_War" title="Russo-Japanese War">Russo-Japanese War</a> pushed the Russians out of the fight for Korea. In <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a> on 26 October 1909, <a href="/wiki/An_Jung-geun" title="An Jung-geun">An Jung-geun</a> assassinated the former <a href="/wiki/Governor-General_of_Korea" title="Governor-General of Korea">Resident-General of Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/It%C5%8D_Hirobumi" title="It\xc5\x8d Hirobumi">It\xc5\x8d Hirobumi</a>, for his role in trying to force Korea into occupation.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japanese_occupation_and_Japan-Korea_Annexation">Japanese occupation and Japan-Korea Annexation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Japanese occupation and Japan-Korea Annexation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Korea_under_Japanese_rule" title="Korea under Japanese rule">Korea under Japanese rule</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_war_crimes" title="Japanese war crimes">Japanese war crimes</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:March_1st_movement.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/March_1st_movement.jpg/220px-March_1st_movement.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/March_1st_movement.jpg/330px-March_1st_movement.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6f/March_1st_movement.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="300" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:March_1st_movement.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe memorial tablet for the <a href="/wiki/March_1st_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="March 1st movement">March 1st movement</a> in Pagoda Park, Seoul</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>In 1910, an already militarily occupied Korea was a forced party to the <a href="/wiki/Japan-Korea_Annexation_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Japan-Korea Annexation Treaty">Japan-Korea Annexation Treaty</a>. The treaty was signed by <a href="/wiki/Lee_Wan-Yong" class="mw-redirect" title="Lee Wan-Yong">Lee Wan-Yong</a>, who was given the General Power of Attorney by the Emperor. However, the Emperor is said to have not actually ratified the treaty according to Yi Tae-jin.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> There is a long dispute whether this treaty was legal or illegal due to its signing under duress, threat of force and bribes.</p>\n<p>Korean resistance to the brutal Japanese occupation<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> was manifested in the nonviolent <a href="/wiki/March_1st_Movement" title="March 1st Movement">March 1st Movement</a> of 1919, during which 7,000 demonstrators were killed by Japanese police and military.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Korean_independence_movement" title="Korean independence movement">Korean liberation movement</a> also spread to neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>.</p>\n<p>Over five million <a href="/wiki/Koreans" title="Koreans">Koreans</a> were conscripted for labour beginning in 1939,<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> and tens of thousands of men were forced into Japan\'s military.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Nearly 400,000 Korean labourers died.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Approximately 200,000 girls and women,<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> mostly from China and Korea, were forced into sexual slavery for the Japanese military.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> In 1993, Japanese Chief Cabinet Secretary <a href="/wiki/Yohei_Kono" class="mw-redirect" title="Yohei Kono">Yohei Kono</a> acknowledged the terrible injustices faced by these euphemistically named "<a href="/wiki/Comfort_women" title="Comfort women">comfort women</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>\n<p>During the Japanese annexation, the Korean language was suppressed in an effort to eradicate Korean national identity. Koreans were forced to take Japanese surnames, known as <a href="/wiki/S%C5%8Dshi-kaimei" title="S\xc5\x8dshi-kaimei">S\xc5\x8dshi-kaimei</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> Traditional <a href="/wiki/Korean_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Korean culture">Korean culture</a> suffered heavy losses, as numerous Korean cultural artifacts were destroyed<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> or taken to Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-Newsweek_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newsweek-146">[146]</a></sup> To this day, valuable Korean artifacts can often be found in Japanese museums or among private collections.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> One investigation by the South Korean government identified 75,311 cultural assets that were taken from Korea, 34,369 in Japan and 17,803 in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. However, experts estimate that over 100,000 artifacts actually remain in Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-Newsweek_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newsweek-146">[146]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-naver_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-naver-148">[148]</a></sup> Japanese officials considered returning Korean cultural properties, but to date<sup id="cite_ref-Newsweek_146-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newsweek-146">[146]</a></sup> this has not occurred.<sup id="cite_ref-naver_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-naver-148">[148]</a></sup> Korea and Japan still dispute the ownership of the <a href="/wiki/Dokdo" class="mw-redirect" title="Dokdo">Dokdo</a>, islets located east of the Korean Peninsula.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></p>\n<p>There was a significant level of emigration to the overseas territories of the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Empire of Japan</a> during the Japanese occupation period, including <a href="/wiki/Korea_under_Japanese_rule" title="Korea under Japanese rule">Korea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> By the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, there were over 850,000 Japanese settlers in Korea.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> After World War II, most of these <a href="/wiki/Overseas_Japanese" class="mw-redirect" title="Overseas Japanese">overseas Japanese</a> repatriated to Japan.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Division">Division</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Korea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Division">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Division_of_Korea" title="Division of Korea">Division of Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korean_conflict" title="Korean conflict">Korean conflict</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Korean_reunification" title="Korean reunification">Korean reunification</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tmulti tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px">\n<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">\n<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_North_Korea.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Flag_of_North_Korea.svg/200px-Flag_of_North_Korea.svg.png" width="200" height="100" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Flag_of_North_Korea.svg/300px-Flag_of_North_Korea.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Flag_of_North_Korea.svg/400px-Flag_of_North_Korea.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="800" /></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Flag of North Korea</div>\n</div>\n<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">\n<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_South_Korea.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Flag_of_South_Korea.svg/200px-Flag_of_South_Korea.svg.png" width="200" height="133" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Flag_of_South_Korea.svg/300px-Flag_of_South_Korea.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Flag_of_South_Korea.svg/400px-Flag_of_South_Korea.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption"
